What are the challenges when it comes to doing Intelligence and Foresighting activities in organizations? Quite a few threads in the discussion forum already highlighted some of those challenges, so when I deal with that topic, I'll pick out those threads and reinforce them.

    After the incentives discussion, I'll share about my consulting project which I did for A*STAR (Agency for Science, Technology and Research) on foresighting and intelligence activities. This was at the national level, but targeting Small and Medium Enterprises (SMEs). Just like how one student shared last week about the "100 Experiments" initiative—how to equip Singapore with AI capabilities—when they did that, they looked at all sectors. My project was similar. It looked at the workforce, schools, higher education institutes, and researchers.

    How many of you know about the **T-Up** (Technology for Enterprise Capability Upgrading) program of A*STAR? Nobody? Basically, I was funded by the NRF (National Research Foundation) to study the effectiveness of T-Up. Maybe when I tell the story, you might become familiar with it. I'll share that after the break. It’s about intelligence and foresighting at the national level, but applied to firms. Right now, to recap the last few weeks: we did TI (Technology Intelligence) at a high level using the conference article. In the next two weeks, I'll give you the theoretical foundation of TI activities—how do you structure an organization, etc. Then we looked at *why* it is important: because it helps firms perform better both in the short and long term. Any questions so far? Very straightforward? Okay, good.

    今天我们的安排是这样的：大家应该都读过**Nucor Corporation（纽克钢铁）**和**Longs Drugs（朗斯药店）**这两个案例了吧？我们今天会围绕这两个案例做一个小组活动，增进大家之间的协作。这部分主要关于激励机制。我们还会讨论一下挑战——在组织中进行情报和预见活动时会面临哪些挑战？讨论区里很多帖子已经提到了一些挑战，稍后讲到这个话题时，我会把那些观点挑出来加以强调。

    讨论完激励机制后，我会分享我为新加坡科技研究局（A*STAR）做的一个咨询项目，是关于预见和情报活动的。虽然是国家层面的项目，但目标对象是中小企业（SME）。就像上周有同学分享的“100个实验”计划——如何让新加坡具备AI能力——这涉及到所有行业。我的项目也是类似的，考察了劳动力、学校、高等教育机构以及研究人员等所有利益相关者。

    在座有多少人知道A*STAR的**T-Up（企业技术升级）**计划？没人知道吗？好吧，基本上这是国家研究基金会（NRF）资助我通过这个计划去研究其有效性的项目。也许等我讲故事的时候你们就熟了。休息回来后我会分享这个案例。它讲的是国家层面的情报预见如何应用于企业。回顾过去几周：我们通过会议文章从高层次了解了技术情报（TI）。接下来的两周，我会讲授TI活动的理论基础——如何构建组织等等。我们还探讨了*为什么*它很重要：因为它能帮助企业在短期和长期都表现得更好。到目前为止有什么问题吗？都很简单明了？好的。

  - [18:11 - 18:31] **Challenges of Foresighting: Student Perspectives**

    When we look at TI or foresighting activities, we know what it is and why it's important. But what are some of the challenges in doing it? That's the topic today. Why do you think it's hard to do foresighting or intelligence in an organization?

    *Student A:* It feels like a textbook concept. There is a disparity; foresighting is actually not being done by the nurses (practitioners) themselves. It's more about awareness and capabilities.

    *Lecturer:* So you're saying it's about not having the in-house awareness and capabilities?

    *Student B:* I also think that these days, organizations try to delegate these tasks to experimental teams. They are trying to catch up to best practices in the industry. Their forecasting is more like "what's going on in the industry currently" and trying to adopt technologies for it. Spending too much on experiments and R&D may not be seen as viable or feasible.

    *Lecturer:* So, one constraint is time and resources. Another is the "wait and watch" attitude—let others do it and then follow.

    *Student C:* More of a practical issue... priorities are all controlled by deadlines. We are always trying to chase these deadlines. Bosses can say "you need to explore R&D," but those are all secondary projects which ultimately no one has time for.

    *Lecturer:* A systemic gap. Always fighting today's battle rather than looking at the future. Very good.

    *Student D:* There are different tiers of people. The lower-level/junior people don't want to change because they can't see the benefit, and change means more work. Freshmen from university might want to rectify the workplace initially, but after half a year, they change (and give up). Middle managers don't want change because it threatens their position. And for the higher level, for them, change might mean "courting death" because the future is unknown. If they go in the wrong direction, they fail.

    *Lecturer:* This summarizes a discussion forum post written by Cheng Heng Lee—are you here? Ah, never mind. That student neatly pointed out that firms are often inward-looking, which constrains their ability to recognize disruptive innovations. People don't want change because they could be displaced. And senior executives are worried about taking the wrong direction—like someone shared last week about a leader throwing the company in a direction that failed dramatically.

    *Student E:* In my company, there is a disconnection in communication between top senior management and the lower level in the field. My company doesn't have a system for reporting these foresighting touchpoints. Sometimes it's up to the person whether they feel something is important enough to report. So there's a mismatch.

    *Lecturer:* Very practical point. Top-down is enforced, and bottom-up is not heard. I'll tell you a practical challenge: I knew a person in an organization who was literally crying, saying, "Hey, this technology is disrupting us!" He attended conferences and knew it was coming. He tried to communicate this to the boss, but no one was ready to hear. He was ignored. So what did he do next time? He didn't bother. He said, "I'll do my minimum to get my salary and move on." According to a survey, many knowledge workers only give a small percentage of their capability to their organizations partly because they are not being heard. That's why knowledge management and having a "sense-making" culture (as Karishma mentioned in the forum) is vital.

    *Student F (SME Context):* For an SME in the energy field, the advantage of being a first mover is not very high. For example, we purchased solar panels at 25-26 cents, and now the rate is 7-10 cents. The first mover advantage isn't really there to justify forecasting. Also, energy markets are volatile. The first person to enter often bears the burden of being the most expensive.

    *Lecturer:* Very good point. For an SME, it can be tricky. Unless you are betting on Intellectual Property (IP) protection to sell to big players, don't be too aggressive on pricing because you cannot beat the big players. You need to play smart.

    当我们审视技术情报或预见活动时，我们知道它的定义和重要性。但在实际操作中有哪些挑战呢？这就是今天的主题。你们觉得为什么在组织里做预见或情报工作这么难？

    *学生A：*这感觉太像教科书里的概念了。现实中有差距，预见工作通常不是由一线人员（比如护士）自己做的。更多是因为缺乏内部的意识和能力。

    *教授：*你是说是因为内部缺乏这种意识和能力？

    *学生B：*我觉得现在很多组织倾向于把这些任务外包给专门的实验团队。他们试图追赶行业的最佳实践。他们的“预测”更多是看“行业里现在发生了什么”，然后试图采用相应的技术。在实验和研发上投入太多，可能被视为不可行或不划算。

    *教授：*所以，一个是时间和资源的限制。另一个是“观望”态度——等别人先做，然后跟风。

    *学生C：*更实际的问题是……优先级都被截止日期控制了。我们总是在赶死线。老板可能会说“你们需要探索研发”，但那些都是次要项目，最终根本没人有时间去做。

    *教授：*这是系统性差距。总是忙着打今天的仗，顾不上看未来。说得很好。

    *学生D：*不同层级的人有不同想法。底层员工不想改变，因为看不到好处，改变意味着更多工作量。刚毕业的新人可能一开始想整顿职场，但半年后就被同化了。中层管理者不想改变，因为这威胁到他们的职位。而对于高层来说，改变可能意味着“找死”，因为未来是未知的。如果方向走错了，公司就完了。

    *教授：*这总结了Cheng Heng Lee在论坛里的帖子——你在吗？算了。那位同学指出，企业往往过于向内看，限制了识别颠覆性创新的能力。员工不想变是因为怕被取代。高管则担心走错方向——就像上周有人分享的，领导者指错方向导致公司惨败。

    *学生E：*在我公司，高层管理和一线人员之间的沟通是断层的。我们没有汇报这些预见触点的系统。有时候汇报不汇报完全看个人感觉。所以存在错配。

    *教授：*非常实际的观点。自上而下是强制执行，自下而上却没人听。我讲个真事：有个员工几乎是哭着喊：“嘿，这项技术正在颠覆我们！”他去参加会议，知道狼来了。他试图告诉老板，但没人听。结果呢？下次他就不费劲了。他说：“我就做最低限度的工作，拿薪水走人。”调查显示，很多知识型员工只发挥了一小部分能力，部分原因就是他们的声音没人听。这就是为什么知识管理和“意义构建”（sense-making，正如Karishma在论坛提到的）至关重要。

    *学生F（中小企业背景）：*对于能源领域的中小企业，先发优势并不大。比如我们在25-26美分时买了太阳能板，现在价格降到了7-10美分。先发者往往要承担最高昂的成本，所以我们对做预见比较怀疑。

    *教授：*这点很好。对中小企业来说这很棘手。除非你是在押注知识产权（IP）保护，然后卖给大公司，否则别在价格上太激进，因为你拼不过大玩家。你得玩得聪明点。

  - [18:32 - 18:35] **Barriers to Foresight Literature**

    If you look at the literature, this is what has been listed as challenges or barriers to foresight. It's not just that foresight capability is lacking. In fact, the capability is often there. The reasons are:

    1. Foresighting is not heard because it is viewed as intangible without much immediate value.
    2. Foresighting is perceived as threatening (as you all mentioned—job loss, change is scary).
    3. Foresight competes for attention. This is one of the most important points.

    Managers are fighting for today rather than looking at the future. In a benchmarking report, researchers asked: "Is a manager more likely to lose his or her job if sales numbers were missed, or if the opportunity to enter a new market was missed?" The answer was overwhelmingly the first one. If I miss that low-hanging fruit (sales targets), I am penalized much more heavily than if I miss a future opportunity. That's why everyone is looking at market size, sales volume, and profit potential *today*, rather than asking: "What will the company be like in 5 or 10 years?"

    For example, CO2 capture is a big topic now for energy efficiency. But how many are really investing in CO2 capture research? There is a famous professor in the Chemical Engineering department at NUS who looks at CO2 capture in the form of gas hydrates. He's a good friend of mine; my son even did an internship in his lab. It's top-notch research, but how long will it take to bring to market? These are far-fetched questions for many firms.

    如果你查阅文献，会发现预见的障碍通常被归结为以下几点。不仅仅是因为缺乏预见能力，事实上能力往往是存在的。真正的原因是：

    1. 预见的声音没人听，因为它被视为无形的，没有太多即时价值。
    2. 预见被视为一种威胁（正如你们提到的——失业、对改变的恐惧）。
    3. 预见在“争夺注意力”。这是最重要的一点。

    管理者都在为今天而战，而不是关注未来。在一份基准报告中，研究人员问：“如果一个经理没完成销售指标，或者错过了进入新市场的机会，哪种情况更容易导致他丢饭碗？”答案压倒性地指向第一种。如果我没摘到“低垂的果实”（当下销售目标），我受到的惩罚远比错过未来机会要重。这就是为什么大家都在关注*当下*的市场规模、销量和利润潜力，而不是问：“5年或10年后公司会变成什么样？”

    比如，二氧化碳（CO2）捕获现在是能源效率的大热门。但有多少人真正投资于CO2捕获的研究？新加坡国立大学化工系有一位著名教授专门研究气体水合物形式的CO2捕获。他是我好朋友，我儿子还在他实验室实习过。这是顶尖的研究，但要推向市场需要多久？对很多公司来说，这些问题太遥远了。

  - [18:35 - 18:39] **Introduction to Group Activity: Incentive Structures**

    If those are the challenges, how can you mitigate them? We'll look at some solutions in the form of **incentives** today. Of course, company culture and structure also matter, which we will cover in the next two weeks. But if you fix incentives, things will often fall in place.

    I had a conversation with an ex-boss yesterday at midnight. He was talking about "eating the frog"—if you have to eat a big frog, eat the brain first, and the rest will fall in place because the brain is the most important thing. Likewise, the easiest thing to work on to mitigate these challenges is fixing incentives—money, stock options, perks. When the annual review comes in July, incentives kick in.

    But are organizations having the right incentives? Is your incentive structure good enough? Before we talk about your organizations, let's look at the two cases: **Longs Drugs** and **Nucor**.

    Start working with your group mates. Build a table.

    1. **Longs Drugs:** What are the different levels? (Department Managers and Store Managers). What is expected of them? What is their KPI and incentive structure?
    2. **Nucor:** What are the levels? (Shop Floor, Mini-Mill Managers, Top Management). What is expected? What are the incentives?

    List them down. Then, analyze: Is this incentive structure good enough? If there are loopholes or gaps, tell me how you can address them by tweaking the structure. There are gaps in Longs Drugs which are well addressed in the Nucor case. I want you to find them.

    You have about 15-20 minutes. Time starts now.

    既然存在这些挑战，我们要如何缓解呢？今天我们通过**激励机制**来探讨解决方案。当然，企业文化和结构也很重要，那是接下来两周的内容。但如果你把激励机制搞对了，事情往往就顺了。

    昨天半夜我和一位前老板聊天。他提到“吃青蛙”理论——如果你非吃一只大青蛙不可，先吃脑子，剩下的就容易了，因为脑子最重要。同样地，要缓解这些挑战，最容易入手的就是修正激励机制——钱、期权、津贴。到了七月份年度评估的时候，激励机制就会发挥作用。

    但是，组织的激励机制设对了吗？你们公司的激励结构够好吗？在讨论你们的公司之前，我们先看看这两个案例：**Longs Drugs**和**Nucor**。

    现在开始小组讨论。做一个表格。

    1. **Longs Drugs：** 有哪些层级？（部门经理和店长）。对他们的期望是什么？KPI和激励结构是什么？
    2. **Nucor：** 有哪些层级？（车间工人、微型钢厂经理、高层管理）。期望是什么？激励是什么？

    把它们列出来。然后分析：这种激励结构够好吗？如果存在漏洞或差距，告诉我如何通过调整结构来解决。Longs Drugs里的一些漏洞在Nucor的案例中解决得很好。我希望你们能发现这一点。

    你们大概有15到20分钟。计时开始。

  - [18:39 - 18:59] **Group Activity Break**

    (Students work in groups to analyze the incentive structures of Longs Drugs and Nucor Corporation.)

    （学生分组讨论Longs Drugs和Nucor Corporation的激励机制。）

  - [18:59 - 19:00] **Chinese New Year Performance Announcement**

    (Returning from break) Just a quick announcement. For the Indian students, you would love this opportunity because you can go to India and come back, but they want a multicultural performance. Is there anyone who is good with any kind of art form? As a group or individuals? Lion dance or anything that can showcase you in the Chinese New Year period (16th to 18th February)? Let me know.

    （休息回来）插播一个通知。印度的同学们，你们可能会喜欢这个机会，因为可以回印度再回来，但他们需要一个多元文化的表演。有没有人擅长某种艺术形式？小组或个人都行？舞狮或者任何能在农历新年期间（2月16日至18日）展示才艺的？请告诉我。

  - [19:00 - 19:04] **Longs Drugs Analysis: Incentives & Strategy**

    Let's come back to **Longs Drugs**. What are the two levels? Department Managers and Store Managers.

    **Department Managers:** They are responsible for the gross margins of their specific department. They decide the mix, shelf space, and reordering.

    **Store Managers:** They have "bottom-line" net profit responsibility. They act like entrepreneurs. Their bonus is based on the store's net profit.

    In Longs Drugs, inventory is mostly either "on the shelf" or "on the road." It's a decentralized model. But moving forward, what is the "con" (disadvantage) of that decentralized approach? The price is going to be higher because you don't have the economies of scale from central purchasing. To mitigate that, they are encouraged to do regional contracts with suppliers to get better prices—consolidating purchases—but not to the extent of a fully centralized chain like Walgreens. They are also encouraged to discuss and brainstorm with other store managers on the profit-maximizing product mix.

    Now, given this strategy, are the incentive structures good enough for what they want to achieve? Or do you think there are gaps? We'll address that.

    让我们回到**Longs Drugs**。有两个层级：部门经理和店长。

    **部门经理：** 他们对本部门的毛利（Gross Margins）负责。他们决定产品组合、货架空间和补货。

    **店长：** 他们对门店的“底线”净利润（Net Profit）负责。他们表现得像企业家。他们的奖金基于门店的净利润。

    在Longs Drugs，库存主要要么“在货架上”，要么“在路上”。这是一个去中心化的模式。但是，这种去中心化方法的缺点是什么？进货价格会更高，因为你没有中央采购的规模经济优势。为了缓解这个问题，公司鼓励他们与供应商签订区域性合同以获得更好的价格——整合采购——但不像Walgreens那样完全中心化。公司还鼓励他们与其他店长讨论和头脑风暴，寻找利润最大化的产品组合。

    现在的关键是，考虑到这个战略，现有的激励结构足以实现他们的目标吗？还是说存在差距？我们会讨论这个问题。

  - [19:04 - 19:07] **Nucor Analysis: Carrots and Sticks**

    Now let's look at **Nucor**. What are the three levels? Shop Floor (Production), Mini-Mill Managers, and Top Management.

    **Shop Floor (Vulcraft Joist Workers):**

    You mentioned "carrots," but there were "sticks" too. What are they?

    - **Carrot (Bonus):** Based on team productivity compared to a 1977 benchmark (tons of finished joists).
    - **Stick (Penalty):** If there is a defect, who fixes it? The team must repair the joist *on their own time*. If they can't repair it, they have to buy it back.
    - **Quality Assurance:** Note that the Quality Assurance inspector is *not* part of the fabrication team. Why? Conflict of interest. If they were part of the team, they might pass faulty products to keep the productivity bonus high. The incentive structure has to be different to avoid this.

    **Mini-Mill Managers:**

    Take note of this detail: Their bonus is based on the **Return on Assets (ROA)** of the *entire* steel division (the mill group), not just their own mill. The case mentions a threshold (earnings above 9% ROE for the pool). And to keep their job, they must achieve at least a 14% ROA in their own mill.

    **Top Management:**

    Their incentives have two components:

    1. Bonus on current profitability (Cash).

    2. Deferred compensation (Stock/Cash held back).

       Why? To ensure they focus on the long term.

    现在看看**Nucor**。三个层级：车间（生产）、微型钢厂经理、高层管理。

    **车间（Vulcraft托梁工人）：**

    你们提到了“胡萝卜”（奖励），但也有“大棒”（惩罚）。是什么？

    - **胡萝卜（奖金）：** 基于团队生产力，对比1977年的基准（成品吨数）。
    - **大棒（惩罚）：** 如果出现缺陷，谁来修？团队必须利用*业余时间*免费修复。如果修不好，他们必须把次品买下来。
    - **质量保证：** 注意，质检员*不*属于制造团队。为什么？为了避免利益冲突。如果他们是一伙的，为了拿高产奖金，可能会放行次品。激励结构必须区分开。

    **微型钢厂经理：**

    注意这个细节：他们的奖金是基于*整个*钢铁部门（钢厂集团）的**资产回报率（ROA）**，而不仅仅是他们自己的钢厂。案例提到有一个门槛（净资产收益率ROE超过9%的部分进入奖金池）。而且为了保住饭碗，他们自己的钢厂必须达到至少14%的ROA。

    **高层管理：**

    他们的激励有两部分：

    1. 基于当前盈利能力的奖金（现金）。

    2. 延期薪酬（扣留的股票/现金）。

       为什么？为了确保他们关注长期发展。

  - [19:07 - 19:13] **Comparative Analysis: Individual vs. Group Incentives**

    Why is Nucor's incentive structure designed like this? Is it good?

    *Student:* For the shop floor, since the bonus is given to the group, there might be free riders. But personally, I think if you put in effort, the bonus grows. It's like sales or insurance—you get what you work for.

    *Lecturer:* Okay, fair enough. But let's look deeper.

    *Student:* I think Longs Drugs has a gap. If the store manager only cares about his own store's profit, he competes with others. There is a misalignment. Incentives should be partially based on the department and partially based on the overall region/store.

    *Lecturer:* Exactly. In the Longs Drugs case, they want stores to share best practices. But if I am a store manager and my bonus is 100% tied to *my* store's profit, do I have any incentive to go and share my secrets with you? No! I want the "Best Store Manager Award." I'll keep it a secret.

    So, having a very short-term or purely individual look at incentives will backfire because there won't be a culture of sharing.

    In the case of **Nucor**, it is neatly done. Every incentive is pitched to **one level higher**.

    - Shop floor: Team productivity (Group level).
    - Mini-mill Managers: Bonus is based on the *Group's* profit (Steel Division), not just their own mill.
    - Top Management: Tied to current profit AND future (deferred compensation).

    This structure forces collaboration. Mini-mill managers meet three times a year to share cost-reduction techniques because *my* bonus depends on *your* mill doing well too. If you only incentivize the individual level, you get superstars but no synergy. You need a balance.

    So when you design an incentive structure, always look at **one level and one level higher**.

    为什么Nucor的激励机制这样设计？它好吗？

    *学生：*对于车间来说，既然奖金是给小组的，可能会有“搭便车”（free riders）的人。但我个人觉得，如果你努力，奖金就会多。这就像销售或保险业——多劳多得。

    *教授：*好吧，有道理。但我们要看得更深一点。

    *学生：*我觉得Longs Drugs有差距。如果店长只关心自己店的利润，他就会和其他店竞争。这里有错位。激励应该部分基于部门表现，部分基于整体区域或门店表现。

    *教授：\*正是如此。在Longs Drugs的案例中，公司希望门店分享最佳实践。但如果我是店长，我的奖金100%挂钩于\*我的*门店利润，我有动力去跟你分享我的秘诀吗？没有！我想要“最佳店长奖”。我会保守秘密。

    所以，目光短浅或纯粹个人的激励机制会适得其反，因为这建立不起分享的文化。

    而在**Nucor**的案例中，设计非常精妙。每一级的激励都锚定在**高一级**的层面。

    - 车间：团队生产力（小组层面）。
    - 微型钢厂经理：奖金基于*集团*利润（钢铁部门），而不只是自己的钢厂。
    - 高层管理：挂钩当前利润以及未来（延期薪酬）。

    这种结构强迫协作。微型钢厂经理每年聚会三次分享降本技术，因为*我的*奖金也取决于*你的*钢厂表现。如果你只激励个人层面，你会得到超级明星，但没有协同效应。你需要平衡。

    所以设计激励结构时，永远要关注**本级和高一级**。

  - [19:13 - 19:15] **Lecturer's Anecdote: NUS Incentive Structure**

    Think about your own organizations. Are your incentives good enough?

    For example, in my department at NUS, we are all faculty members. Many times I don't even come to the office because I work on my research, teaching, and mentoring. I work with collaborators outside Singapore (Europe, Canada) more than with colleagues here.

    It's good for *me*—I get grants. But is it good for collegiality within the department? No.

    So, my Head of Department created an incentive: "If there is interdisciplinary research happening *within* the group, I will park a specific pool of money for that research." That is an incentive for me to talk to my colleague and say, "Hey, can we go and tap into this pool together?"

    It brings synergy. Without it, individually we do well, but when an external audit comes, they ask: "How good are you as a group?" That matters for retention too. If there is no friendship, no collegiality, why would I want to stay here in the long run?

    想以此反思你们自己的组织。激励机制够好吗？

    比如，在新加坡国立大学我们系，大家都是教员。很多时候我甚至不去办公室，因为我忙着做研究、教学和指导学生。我跟新加坡以外（欧洲、加拿大）的合作者共事的时间，比跟这里同事共事的时间还多。

    这对*我*个人是好事——我拿到了资助。但这有利于系里的同僚情谊吗？并没有。

    所以，我的系主任设立了一个激励措施：“如果组内有*跨学科*研究，我会专门拨一笔钱支持。”这就是我去跟同事说话的动力：“嘿，我们要不要一起去申请这笔钱？”

    这带来了协同效应。没有这个，我们单打独斗都不错，但外部审计来的时候会问：“作为一个团队，你们有多强？”这也关系到留任率。如果没有友谊，没有同僚情谊，我为什么要长期留在这里？

  - [19:15 - 19:34] **Student Discussion: Sales vs. Finance & Performance Ratings**

    *Lecturer:* How many of you are still assessed on a scale of 1 to 5? Is it comprehensive enough?

    *Student A (Medical/Research):* For us, it involves bedside research, publishing... lots of expectations and no time to do it.

    *Lecturer:* You're being diplomatic. Okay, for me, if you do your core job well (teaching, foundation), you get a standard grade. What brings the "A" or "A Star" are the **CCAs** (Co-Curricular Activities)—additional activities the boss appoints to stretch you. But are you compensated well enough for that extra work? Or is it just a hygiene factor?

    *Student B (Sales vs. Finance Conflict):* I want to share a specific gap in my sales department. Salespeople get commission based on **Purchase Orders (POs)**. So they care about getting as many clients as possible. But then the Finance department has to chase these clients to collect payment. The salespeople say, "We did our part, we sold it. Collection is your problem." But they bring in bad clients. The incentive should not just be on the PO amount, but on the *actual amount collected*.

    *Lecturer:* Very true. This happens in many companies—Singapore, Thailand, everywhere. "I got the client, you deal with the mess." I can totally relate.

    *Student C (SME vs. MNC):* I've worked in a foreign MNC, a local MNC, and a small SME.

    - **SME:** Usually just salary. No deferred compensation. Bonus is random—"profit is up, here's a bonus." Not structured.

    - **Foreign MNC:** Two-way appraisal. Different levels of grading.

    - **Local MNC:** Somewhere in between.

      But in an SME, it's about managing your boss. One boss handles everyone. It's subjective.

    *Lecturer:* Yes, in an SME, you don't have the layers, but the chance of being noticed is higher. If you do well, you get more responsibility (and maybe more pay). In Singapore/Asia, "reward" often means "more work," right? But you get visibility.

    Regarding appraisals, some MNCs do **two-way appraisals** (you rate the boss too). But research shows a bias—female employees often rate female bosses lower than male bosses. I've seen this in my own class ratings too! Male students gave me 4.9, female students gave me 4.3. I was like, "What the hell did I do?" So, bias exists.

    *教授：*你们中有多少人还在用1到5分的评分制？这够全面吗？

    *学生A（医疗/研究）：*对我们要来说，涉及临床研究、发表论文……期望很高，但没时间做。

    *教授：*你说话很外交辞令啊。好吧，对我来说，如果你做好了核心工作（教学、基础），你只能拿个标准分。想要拿到“A”或“A星”，得靠**CCA**（辅助课程活动）——老板指派给你的额外任务，用来磨练你。但这些额外工作有足够的补偿吗？还是仅仅被当作理所当然？

    *学生B（销售与财务的冲突）：\*我想分享销售部门的一个具体差距。销售人员的提成是基于**采购订单（PO）**的。所以他们只管拉尽可能多的客户。然后财务部门得去追着这些人讨债。销售人员说：“我们完事了，卖出去了。收款是你们的问题。”结果他们招来一堆烂客户。激励机制不应该只基于订单金额，而应该基于\*实际收回的款项*。

    *教授：*太真实了。这在很多公司都发生——新加坡、泰国，到处都是。“我拉来了客户，烂摊子归你。”我完全能感同身受。

    *学生C（中小企业 vs 跨国公司）：*我在外资跨国公司、本土跨国公司和小型中小企业都做过。

    - **中小企业：** 通常只有死工资。没有延期薪酬。奖金很随意——“今年利润不错，发个红包。”没有结构化。

    - **外资跨国公司：** 双向评估。分级很细。

    - **本土跨国公司：** 介于两者之间。

      但在中小企业，主要是看怎么“管理”你的老板。一个老板管所有人。很主观。

    *教授：*是的，中小企业没有那么多层级，但你被注意到的机会也更高。如果你做得好，你会得到更多责任（也许还有更多钱）。在新加坡或亚洲，“奖励”往往意味着“更多工作”，对吧？但你能得到曝光度。

    关于评估，有些跨国公司做**双向评估**（你也给老板打分）。但研究显示存在偏见——女性员工给女性老板打的分通常比给男性老板低。我在我自己的课程评分里也见过！男学生给我4.9分，女学生给我4.3分。我想：“我到底做错了什么？”所以，偏见是存在的。

  - [19:34 - 19:37] **New Employee Probation & Unspoken Rules**

    *Student:* There is an unspoken rule regarding new employees. Even if you perform well, you get a "C" grade (average) because you are new. It's like a queue system. "I took the fall last year, so you take it this year."

    *Lecturer:* When you say "new," how new? Less than one year?

    *Student:* Yes.

    *Lecturer:* That's fair enough. It's too early to judge. Maybe you came in with a lot of energy, working 24/7, but if I let you work for a year, you might lose steam and deteriorate. They want to see you perform consistently. So if you get an average grade initially, don't worry. If they didn't tell you to "buck up," it's a good sign. Continue the good work, and you should be remunerated the subsequent year.

    *学生：*对于新员工有个潜规则。即使你表现得很好，你也只能拿个“C”档（平均分），因为你是新人。这就像排队一样。“去年我背了锅，今年轮到你了。”

    *教授：*你说的“新”，有多新？不到一年？

    *学生：*是的。

    *教授：\*那很公平。现在下定论太早了。也许你刚来时精力充沛，全天候工作，但如果我让你干满一年，你可能就泄气了，表现下滑。他们想看到你表现的\*持续性*。所以如果起初拿到平均分，别担心。如果他们没叫你“加把劲”，那就是好兆头。继续努力，第二年你应该会得到回报的。

  - [19:37 - 19:38] **Closing Advice: Do Not Get Fired**

    Just because I told you about incentive structures and two-way communication, don't go and argue with your boss anyhow. "Annie (Lecturer) taught me there should be two-way communication, I demand a change!" Don't get fired and talk. I put a disclaimer: I am not responsible for your own actions!

    Same thing for the SMEs here. Don't go and start a massive "Intelligence and Foresighting Department" because it's going to backfire big time. You don't have the bandwidth. Look for blind spots, weak signals from others, and listen to your customers. Do that. Don't get fired and come to me next class.

    We'll take a break and come back to look at **T-Up** (The A*STAR study).

    最后提醒一句，虽然我讲了激励结构和双向沟通，别回去就随便跟老板吵架。“Annie（老师）教我要双向沟通，我要求改革！”别搞得被炒鱿鱼了再来说。我先免责声明：我不对你们的行为负责！

    在座的中小企业也一样。别回去就搞个庞大的“情报与预见部门”，那会彻底搞砸的。你们没有那个带宽（资源）。去寻找盲点，捕捉别人的微弱信号，倾听客户的声音。做这些就够了。别搞到被炒了下节课来找我哭。

    我们要休息一下，回来后讲**T-Up**（A*STAR的研究案例）。

  - [19:51 - 19:57] **Singapore's Transition to a Knowledge-Based Economy**

    The Singapore government commissioned this study, specifically the Economic Development Board (EDB), which funded the whole initiative to help Small and Medium Enterprises (SMEs). To understand why, you need the background. Those who have been in Singapore for a long time know that up until 1990, the focus was on industrialization. The economy was skill-based; polytechnics and engineering skills were heavily emphasized. While this helped Singapore do well, 1990 marked the transition into a **Knowledge-Based Economy (KBE)**. We became "knowledge-based workers."

    Now, who is a knowledge-based worker? Forget the complex theories. A knowledge worker is defined by the ability to reflect. If you do a job—any activity—and you are able to step back after performing the task and ask yourself: "Could I have done this better? Did I do anything wrong? Had I had a specific tool, would I have performed this job better?"—that differentiates a knowledge worker. It’s about reflective learning. That is why they generalize white-collar workers as knowledge workers, because they are assumed to have that level of critical thinking. Even in the US, some people refuse to become managers because they want to remain champions in their technical area; they are knowledge workers too.

    I mention this because it applies to your personal life. Every year on December 30th or 31st, I ask myself: "How did I spend the last year? What did I achieve monthly? What could I do better?" If you can do that for your last semester, you are already practicing it. After 1990, Singapore realized that skills alone wouldn't help because neighboring countries were becoming skillful and offering lower costs. To remain competitive, the government started pumping money into research. The National Science and Technology Board (NSTB) was established in 1990 and renamed **A\*STAR (Agency for Science, Technology and Research)** around 2001/2002. A*STAR became the breeding place for knowledge workers, dramatically increasing the number of PhDs and researchers.

    这项研究是由新加坡政府，特别是经济发展局（EDB）委托并全额资助的，旨在帮助中小企业。为了理解原因，我得先讲讲背景。在新加坡待得久的人都知道，直到1990年，我们的重心都在工业化。那是基于技能的经济，理工学院和工程技能备受推崇。这确实让新加坡发展得不错，但1990年标志着我们向**知识型经济（KBE）**的转型。我们变成了“知识型工作者”。

    那么，谁是知识型工作者？别管那些复杂的理论。知识型工作者的定义在于反思能力。如果你在做完一项工作——任何工作——之后，能够退一步问自己：“我能做得更好吗？我做错了什么吗？如果我有某种工具，这工作会不会做得更出色？”——这就区分了你是不是知识型工作者。关键在于反思性学习。这就是为什么白领通常被归类为知识型工作者，因为人们假设他们具备这种批判性思维。甚至在美国，有些人拒绝升职做经理，因为他们只想在技术领域做顶尖专家，他们也是知识型工作者。

    我提这个是因为这关乎你们的个人生活。每年的12月30日或31日，我都会问自己：“这一年我怎么过的？我每个月达成了什么？哪里可以改进？”如果你能对上学期做同样的复盘，你就已经入门了。1990年后，新加坡意识到光靠技能不够了，因为邻国的技术也在进步，而且成本更低。为了保持竞争力，政府开始向研究领域注入大量资金。国家科技局（NSTB）于1990年成立，并在2001/2002年左右更名为**A\*STAR（新加坡科技研究局）**。A*STAR成了知识型人才的孵化器，博士和研究人员的数量急剧增加。

  - [19:57 - 20:06] **The SME Gap and the GET-Up Initiative (T-Up)**

    Around 2000, the government realized a problem. While there was massive investment in upskilling the workforce and research, SMEs were not growing well. About 65% of employment came from the SME sector, but in terms of nominal value-added, they were lagging. When asked why, SMEs complained: "I'm fighting for my bread and butter today. I'm not thinking about the future. And even if I want to be futuristic, I don't have R&D capabilities. PhDs want to work for large institutes, not SMEs."

    So, in 2003, the government launched a program called **GET-Up (Growing Enterprises through Technology Upgrade)**. It had several components.

    1. **OTR (Operation Technology Roadmap):** **Rob Phaal** from the University of Cambridge, the guru on roadmapping, came to Singapore to train A*STAR staff. Since then, A*STAR has a group that goes into SMEs for 1-2 weeks to chart their R&D strategy.
    2. **Technical Advisors:** SMEs can tap into A*STAR experts for advice.
    3. **T-Up (Technology for Enterprise Capability Upgrading):** This is the key component. It is a secondment program. If an SME needs a researcher, A*STAR sends their employee to the SME.
       - **Year 1:** The researcher is fully paid by the government (EDB). The SME gets "free labor" and high-level knowledge.
       - **Year 2:** The SME must pay roughly 25-50% of the salary.
       - **Goal:** By the end, the SME becomes invested and hopefully hires the researcher permanently or the researcher returns to A*STAR with industry experience. It is essentially headhunting from A*STAR to SMEs to make them futuristic.

    在2000年左右，政府意识到了一个问题。虽然在提升劳动力技能和研究方面投入巨大，但中小企业的发展却不尽如人意。大约65%的就业来自中小企业，但就名义增值而言，它们却掉队了。当被问及原因时，中小企业抱怨道：“我每天都在为生计奔波，哪有空想未来？就算我想搞前沿技术，我也没那个研发能力。博士们都想去大机构，没人愿意来中小企业。”

    因此，政府在2003年推出了**GET-Up（企业技术升级）**计划。它包含几个部分：

    1. **OTR（运营技术路线图）：** 剑桥大学的**Rob Phaal**，他是技术路线图领域的泰斗，亲自来新加坡培训A*STAR的员工。从那时起，A*STAR有一个团队专门入驻中小企业1-2周，帮它们制定研发战略。
    2. **技术顾问：** 中小企业可以利用A*STAR的专家资源获取建议。
    3. **T-Up（企业能力提升技术计划）：** 这是核心部分，即借调计划。如果中小企业需要研究员，A*STAR会派员工去。
       - **第一年：** 研究员的薪水由政府（EDB）全额支付。中小企业获得了“免费劳动力”和高端知识。
       - **第二年：** 中小企业必须承担大约25-50%的薪水。
       - **目标：** 最终，中小企业会对此投入，希望它们能正式聘用该研究员，或者研究员带着行业经验回到A*STAR。这本质上是从A*STAR向中小企业“挖墙脚”，以帮助企业面向未来。

  - [20:06 - 20:15] **Evaluating Effectiveness: Metrics and The "Kill" Success**

    Around 2015, the government wanted to know if this investment paid off economically. I was funded by the NRF to study the effectiveness of T-Up. How do you measure effectiveness?

    - **Financials:** Revenue growth, cost savings.
    - **Innovation:** Number of patents, new products, process improvements.
    - **Growth:** Scaling up, job creation.
    - **Ecosystem:** Are there more entrepreneurs?

    One of you mentioned in the forum that not all foresighting projects result in an executable project. Does killing a project mean failure? No. Discontinuing a project is **cost saving**. It is an opportunity cost saved by not investing in a dead end. So, for SMEs, process improvement or avoiding bad investments is a huge success metric.

    We also looked at a model by **Bozeman** called the "Contingency Effectiveness Model." It says effectiveness depends on: *Who* is transferring? *What* is being transferred? *To whom*? *How* (human mobility vs. patent)? And the *Context*. This guided our study.

    2015年左右，政府想知道这项投资在经济上是否得到了回报。我获得了国家研究基金会（NRF）的资助来研究T-Up的有效性。怎么衡量有效性呢？

    - **财务：** 营收增长，成本节约。
    - **创新：** 专利数量，新产品，流程改进。
    - **增长：** 规模扩大，创造就业。
    - **生态系统：** 是否诞生了更多企业家？

    你们有人在论坛里提到，并非所有的预见项目都会转化为执行项目。那砍掉一个项目算失败吗？不。终止项目实际上是**成本节约**。这意味着你避免了在死胡同里投入资源，从而节省了机会成本。所以对中小企业来说，流程改进或规避错误投资也是巨大的成功指标。

    我们还参考了**Bozeman**提出的“权变有效性模型”。该模型认为有效性取决于：*谁*在转移？转移了*什么*？转移*给谁*？*如何*转移（通过人员流动还是专利）？以及*背景*。这指导了我们的研究。

  - [20:15 - 20:24] **The Matchmaking Failure & The Readiness Matrix**

    We conducted 41 interviews. We found discrepancies: some SMEs were happy, others had nasty things to say. Some researchers felt it was the worst experience ever. We did paired interviews (SME boss + Researcher) to understand why.

    We realized the problem was **bad matchmaking** by the SME Office. It’s like a date funded by the EDB—if matched poorly, it's a waste.

    We developed a framework to explain this based on **Technology Readiness Level (TRL)** and **Demand Readiness Level**.

    - **Low Readiness SME:** Has no clue what they want, no in-house R&D.
    - **High Readiness SME:** Has done in-house R&D, knows exactly what capability they lack.
    - **High TRL Technology:** Mature, plug-and-play, proven.
    - **Low TRL Technology:** Embryonic, conceptual, lab-stage.

    **The Finding:**

    - If you send a **High TRL** (mature) technology, it works for *any* SME (Low or High readiness). It's plug-and-play.
    - If you send a **Low TRL** (conceptual) technology to a **Low Readiness SME**, it is a disaster. The SME boss expects immediate sales, but the researcher is still theorizing.
    - **Low TRL technology only works with High Readiness SMEs.**
    - *Example:* One enlightened SME boss (himself a researcher) took a researcher for a cutting-edge project. In two years, no product, no patent. But the boss was happy because they made progress on a complex idea. He understood the R&D timeline.

    We showed this matrix to the A*STAR board, and they immediately asked: "How many wrong matchmakings have we done?"

    我们进行了41次访谈。我们发现了差异：有些中小企业很高兴，有些则骂骂咧咧。有些研究员觉得这是史上最糟的经历。我们做了配对访谈（中小企业老板+研究员）来找出原因。

    我们意识到问题出在中小企业办公室的**糟糕配对**上。这就像一场由EDB出钱的相亲——如果配对得不好，就是浪费。

    我们基于**技术就绪指数（TRL）\**和\**需求就绪指数**开发了一个框架来解释这一点。

    - **低就绪度中小企业：** 完全不知道自己想要什么，没有内部研发能力。
    - **高就绪度中小企业：** 做过内部研发，清楚知道自己缺什么能力。
    - **高TRL技术：** 成熟，即插即用，经过验证。
    - **低TRL技术：** 萌芽期，概念性，实验室阶段。

    **研究发现：**

    - 如果你输送**高TRL**（成熟）技术，它适用于*任何*中小企业（无论就绪度高低）。因为是即插即用的。
    - 如果你把**低TRL**（概念性）技术输送给**低就绪度中小企业**，那就是场灾难。老板期望立竿见影的销量，而研究员还在搞理论。
    - **低TRL技术只适用于高就绪度中小企业。**
    - *例子：* 有位开明的中小企业老板（自己也是研究员）接收了一名研究员做一个前沿项目。两年里，没产品，没专利。但老板很高兴，因为他们在一个复杂的概念上取得了进展。他理解研发的时间周期。

    当我们向A*STAR董事会展示这个矩阵时，他们立马问：“我们要搞砸了多少次配对？”

  - [20:24 - 20:30] **Economic Impact: The Inverted U-Curve**

    To prove this economically, we used a **Control Group**: SMEs that applied for T-Up but were denied. We compared their performance against those who got it.

    - **Result:** T-Up generally increased revenue, R&D spending, and employment.
    - **Intangible Benefits:** Even without a product, SMEs felt safer having a "Big Brother" (A*STAR) to talk to.
    - **The Inverted U-Curve:** We found that as the number of T-Up secondments increases, SME output increases *up to a point*. Beyond that point, it plateaus. Why? Because the SME becomes self-sufficient in R&D. They don't need the government crutch anymore.
    - **Policy Implication:** If an SME keeps coming back for more T-Up support, at some point, A*STAR should stop helping, because they have already graduated.

    为了从经济角度证明这一点，我们使用了**对照组**：那些申请了T-Up但被拒绝的中小企业。我们将它们的表现与获得支持的企业进行了对比。

    - **结果：** T-Up总体上增加了营收、研发投入和就业。
    - **无形收益：** 即使没有做出产品，中小企业也觉得有个“老大哥”（A*STAR）可以咨询，更有安全感。
    - **倒U型曲线：** 我们发现，随着T-Up借调次数的增加，中小企业的产出在*一定程度上*是增加的。但超过那个点后，增长就停滞了。为什么？因为中小企业在研发上已经能够自给自足了。它们不再需要政府的拐杖。
    - **政策启示：** 如果一家中小企业不断回来申请T-Up支持，到了某个点，A*STAR应该停止资助，因为它们已经“毕业”了。

  - [20:31 - 20:40] **Researcher Benefits & Conclusion**

    The third question was: What's in it for the researcher? Many hated it. They felt "out of sight, out of mind"—friends got promoted while they were away, and they returned to just a $500 certificate.

    - **Success Story:** One female researcher, a scholar from Cambridge, was sent to an SME to make a Hand, Foot, and Mouth disease toolkit. The SME was small, so she was forced to learn *everything*: patents, white space analysis, working with lawyers, cleaning the office, talking to customers. She gained skills she never learned in her PhD. She is now ready to start her own company.
    - **Conclusion:** Researchers only gain these business/commercialization skills if the project is successful. If they just "time pass," they gain nothing.

    **Final Takeaway for you as Analysts:**

    1. **Frame the Question:** Don't just "study impact." Work with stakeholders (EDB, A*STAR, SMEs) to define what value means to each.
    2. **Methodology:** Be ready to use rigorous economic analysis (like QCA - Qualitative Comparative Analysis) for stakeholders like EDB who want hard numbers, while using stories for others.
    3. **Context:** Don't generalize. Success looks different in different contexts.

    Next week, we will look at **Theories on Technology Intelligence**. It will be theory-heavy. Thank you.

    第三个问题是：这对研究员有什么好处？很多人讨厌这个项目。他们觉得“眼不见心不烦”——朋友们都在升职，而他们被外派，回来只拿到一张500块的证书。

    - **成功案例：** 有位女性研究员，剑桥归来的学者，被派去一家中小企业开发手足口病检测试剂盒。因为公司很小，她被迫学习*所有东西*：专利、空白点分析、跟律师打交道、甚至打扫办公室、跟客户谈生意。她学到了读博期间从未学到的技能。她现在已经准备好自己开公司了。
    - **结论：** 研究员只有在项目成功时才能获得这些商业/商业化技能。如果只是“混日子”，他们什么也得不到。

    **给各位分析师的最终建议：**

    1. **界定问题：** 别只是“研究影响”。要与利益相关者（EDB、A*STAR、中小企业）合作，定义对各方而言什么是价值。
    2. **方法论：** 准备好使用严谨的经济分析（如QCA——定性比较分析）来应对像EDB这样看重硬数据的利益相关者，同时用故事打动其他人。
    3. **背景：** 不要一概而论。在不同背景下，成功的样子是不同的。

    下周，我们将探讨**技术情报理论**。理论性会比较强。谢谢大家。
## Week 4

And all the case studies, in fact all the models that are derived, are out of case studies observing folks—how do they do foresighting and intelligence? And that's how the theory is formed. So that will give you a flavor for what things really happen all over the framework.     好了，今天的话题更多是关于流程本身的。为什么我们要这样做，我会讲到。事实上，这也是今天挑战的一部分。像往常一样，我们会总结上周的内容，然后讨论技术情报（TI）的理论模型，它会用我稍后展示的一张图表来讲解所有这些阶段。我问一下：你们读那些文章了吗？能看懂吗？似懂非懂？好的，这是有原因的，因为你们还没有接触到所有的概念。所以在今天的讲座之后，如果你们再读一遍，就会更明白了。

    但我给你们文章是为了让你们明白，我们今天涵盖的内容不仅仅是在书本里，它确实发生在现实生活中。所有的案例研究，事实上所有推导出的模型，都是来自于对人们的观察案例——他们是如何做预见和情报的？理论就是这样形成的。所以这会让你们对框架下实际发生的事情有一种直观的感受。就过去两周而言，我们探讨了什么是技术情报，为什么它很重要，做这件事有哪些挑战，以及如何实现它。当然，我们也看了 AI Singapore 和你们的案例，这两者都与如何评估某些决策的有效性有关，主要是为了帮助公司面向未来。

    **Chen Zhengdao**, you mentioned how in operational aspects—because intelligence also has to do with operational aspects—you were mentioning about how they are focusing on the short term and not the long term. This is one of the very practical challenges with all companies. They think operational people need not do intelligence, but that's not how it works. Even for operations, you need to be futuristic. For example, one of the projects which I recently was also part of a panel for, is from an **A\*STAR** student working on how warehouses can implement multifunctional robots. Typically, they have robots for warehouses, right? For lifting and then sending to a dock. But what happens is they have one specific robot for one purpose, so many times it's sitting idle when the second move is happening. So he was thinking about implementing this multifunctional robot which can handle multiple jobs if it's programmed. But for that to function well, even the software needs to be updated behind the scene and it is AI-based, learning-based as well. So when he posed this idea—why not implement this for a warehouse?—there was a big stigma happening in the warehousing. Why should we? Because it's not cheap. And what is the second challenge? One is, it's not cheap. Second one is... it exists, it's not that they are designing it, they are adopting it. But you're nearing the answer. What are the challenges with implementation? It's at a very embryonic stage of evolution. Now, if the technology is not mature and you want to go ahead and implement it, of course there'll be implementation challenges, how to program it, lots of challenges will be there. So the boss was thinking, does it really make sense? Should I invest in this? So you have to do a cost-benefit analysis and then you have to look at how long the impact will take for the warehouse to make me realize benefits. So he was thinking futuristic for his operational part. So even operations folks cannot look at only short term; they have to be futuristic as well. That is one point I want to emphasize.

    And **Li Jun**, yeah, so you shared about HR-related foresighting, which was very interesting. It's a very different perspective. The KPIs are all measured about how fast you hire, whom you hired, etc. But what is the wrong on the corner of it? Are they sticking along? If they leave the organization, all the effort spent is going to waste. How did you balance the two aspects? In any case, I know you mentioned that they measure it, but how did they take care of incentives? So what—I don't know how many of you looked into it—but what she mentioned is, yeah, HR is hiring people, but if the work culture in the organization sucks, people leave. Now, can the incentive be tied to retaining people as well? To some extent they should, but are they fully responsible for the action? HR designed everything, also, that's what you think. But actually it doesn't happen that way, right? The projects are running the way they are run. So that is where the multifunctional aspects come into the picture.

    This is very similar to what **Karthik** has mentioned as well. They collect a lot of data from that side, but the data is only visible to middle managers. They are playing the role of intelligence gatekeepers. So we'll talk about it today. Well, if it's not even visible to the nurses, how are they going to act on it? Right, a very practical challenge.     And there were two things which really stood out in terms of interesting context. One is from **Yi Jun**. Sorry. So he works in the security-related area. So in this, how do you measure foresight? It's always about what is *not* done. It's like—I work in pharma. When it comes to cancer, curing cancer is like, okay, whether the cells are killed or not. It's a binary disease. But in the case of Alzheimer's, that is very, very difficult because it's not like a binary decision; only you get to know after so many years that Alzheimer's is kicking in. And even after it kicks in, it's more about symptom addressing or mitigating rather than curing it. Okay, so your situation of security is like that. Foresighting is about when you do foresighting and act on it, people don't see it. So how do you assess the impact of it? That is where backtracking comes in—because if a calamity or disaster were to happen, then you can estimate the cost and what is the disaster and all everything. And you prevented it. How are you going to assess the impact? It's a very practical challenge.

    现在回到关于什么是预见、为什么它很重要以及有哪些挑战的 Logbook 上来。其实，我想用你们上周的论坛讨论作为一个例子，来展示我期望你们做什么。如果你参考那个例子或者讨论论坛里的问题，基本上是在看如何捕捉公司中预见活动的影响。短期的，长期的。以及用什么衡量标准来捕捉这种影响？是的，不仅如此。最后还有一点——建议。是的，如果有任何陷阱，你如何规避它？你如何改进它本身？

    好的，所以如果你仔细看，这就是我对你们 Logbook 中要求涵盖的五个主题的期望。首先，介绍一下公司的背景或者你正在从事的工作，以及你关注的 TI（技术情报）活动是什么。一些没有工作经验的学生试图把当天讲座的内容基本复述一遍。如果你没有工作经验，这样做是可以的。你不会因此挂科。所以你能做的基本水平至少是正确地提到课堂上讲过的内容。好吗？对于那些有经验并且想更多思考自己经验的人，你不必一定要涵盖讲座中讲过的内容，但至少给出一个概要，说明你要谈论这个特定的话题，这样我就知道是什么了。一旦你这样做了，给出你的背景以及该背景下的 TI 或预见经验。如果有问题，你如何规避？基于你在课堂上学到的东西，你如何改进它？甚至在应用了所有课堂所学之后，如果你认为仍然存在问题，并且你有办法规避它——这就更进一步了。

    Level 1 只是复述课堂上提到的内容。Level 2 是讲述你的预见经验，如果有什么挑战，是什么？有没有办法规避？Level 4 是：即使应用了所有这些，你认为仍然存在问题，并且这需要一些跳出框框的思考，你对此进行了研究并尝试解决它。这是最高水平。好的，这基本上是为了区分普通、高于平均水平和优秀的学生。Logbook 就是这样评分的。

    你们在讨论论坛里做的已经很好了。你们很多人提到了自己公司发生的事情，潜在的挑战是什么，以及如何克服它们，这很好。很多人提到了短期和长期的 KPI，有些人甚至提到了中期，这很好。通常确实有短期、中期和长期之分。所以这部分有些人已经涵盖了。接下来我想强调几点，关于以后如何进行论坛讨论——不是 Logbook，而是讨论论坛。我希望你们在彼此的想法基础上进行构建。好的，这又回到了我之前的课。如果我看讨论论坛，有太多“我也一样（me too）”的想法。我不希望发生这种情况。

    **Chen Zhengdao**，你提到了在运营方面——因为情报也与运营方面有关——你提到他们如何关注短期而不是长期。这是所有公司面临的一个非常实际的挑战。他们认为运营人员不需要做情报，但事实并非如此。即使对于运营，你也需要面向未来。例如，我最近参与评审的一个项目，来自一位 **A\*STAR** 的学生，他研究仓库如何实施多功能机器人。通常，仓库里有机器人，对吧？用于举升然后送到码头。但问题是，他们有一种特定用途的机器人，所以很多时候当进行第二个动作时，它是闲置的。所以他在考虑实施这种多功能机器人，如果经过编程，它可以处理多项工作。但为了使其良好运行，甚至后台软件也需要更新，而且它是基于 AI 和学习的。所以当他提出这个想法时——为什么不在仓库实施这个？——在仓储领域有一个很大的顾虑。我们为什么要这样做？因为它不便宜。第二个挑战是什么？一个是不便宜。第二个是……它已经存在了，并不是他们在设计它，而是在采用它。但你已经接近答案了。实施的挑战是什么？它处于非常早期的演变阶段。如果技术还不成熟，你想推进并实施它，当然会有实施挑战，如何编程，会有很多挑战。所以老板在想，这真的有意义吗？我应该投资这个吗？所以你必须做成本效益分析，然后你必须看仓库需要多长时间才能让我意识到效益。所以他在为他的运营部分做面向未来的思考。所以即使是运营人员也不能只看短期，他们也必须面向未来。这是我想强调的一点。

    还有 **Li Jun**，是的，你分享了关于人力资源相关的预见，这非常有趣。这是一个非常不同的视角。我是说，不像技术、产品那些，她关注的是人力资源相关的预见，这是非常非常实际的。KPI 都是衡量你招聘多快，招了谁等等。但这背后的问题是什么？他们留下来了吗？如果他们离开组织，所有的努力都浪费了。你如何平衡这两个方面？无论如何，我知道你提到他们衡量它，但他们如何处理激励措施？所以——我不知道你们中有多少人研究过这个——但她提到的是，是的，HR 在招人，但如果组织的工作文化很烂，人们就会离开。现在，激励措施能不能也与留住人挂钩？在某种程度上应该，但他们对这个行为负全责吗？HR 设计了一切，你是这么想的。但实际上并非如此，对吧？项目是按既定方式运行的。所以这就是多功能方面发挥作用的地方。

    这与 **Karthik** 提到的非常相似。他们从那边收集了很多数据，但这些数据只有中层管理者可见。他们扮演着情报守门人的角色。所以我们要谈谈这个。如果护士甚至都看不见这些数据，她们怎么根据数据行动呢？对，一个非常实际的挑战。这就是可见性和沟通，以及我们要谈论的——**组织的智能（Intelligence of an Organization）** 与 **组织性智能（Organizational Intelligence）**——整件事都与文化结构有关，这与我们今天今天要讲的内容紧密相关。Karthik 也提到了类似的事情，它仍然是一个“黑匣子”。战略思维和愿景，这非常相似。展望未来，我希望你们在这样的事情上进行构建。当然，不要只提到短期、长期、KPI 之类的，而是开始给出有趣的转折。因为一旦你提到短期和长期，几个人会说这是显而易见的，对吧？然后在随后的讨论论坛中，开始在这个想法上构建，说：“你知道短期和长期……它是如何与不同职能交织在一起的？它如何变得可见？”

    还有两件事在有趣的背景方面真的很突出。一个是来自 **Yi Jun**。抱歉。他在安全相关领域工作。所以在这种情况下，你如何衡量预见？这总是关于什么 *没有* 被做。就像——我在制药行业工作。当谈到癌症时，治愈癌症就像是，好吧，细胞是否被杀死了。这是一种二元疾病。但在阿尔茨海默病的情况下，那是通过非常非常困难的，因为它不是二元决定；只有在很多年后你才知道阿尔茨海默病发作了。即使发作后，更多的是关于症状处理或缓解，而不是治愈它。好的，所以你的安全情况就是那样。预见是关于当你做预见并采取行动时，人们看不到它。所以你如何评估它的影响？这就是回溯发挥作用的地方——因为如果灾难发生了，你可以估算成本和灾难是什么等等。而你阻止了它。你打算如何评估影响？这是一个非常实际的挑战。

    这与 **Meredith** 早些时候分享的非常相似。它是关于关闭一个项目；这本身就是预见。这为组织节省了多少成本。所以这些方面是非常非常有趣的。她还分享了一些关于 COVID-19 的东西，创业文化是如何被培育的。它没有立即启动，但它仍然开启了浪潮。所以当你们做讨论论坛时，如果你想要更好的分数，好吗：不要以为写 *点什么* 就能在以后获得更多分数。关键在于你如何在彼此的想法上进行构建，并对抛出的想法给出有趣的角度，而不是仅仅是“我也一样”的那种谈话。清楚了吗？所以如果你想在 Logbook 和讨论论坛上得分高，试着理解这一点。这样线程的数量变得可控。阅读和学习变得更加愉快。对此有什么问题吗？这将迫使你们思考——拥有这种更高阶的思维——这也会对你们的 Logbook 有帮助。对。否则就像“噢拿这个，放这里”，是行不通的。好了。我想听听那些没有经验的人的意见。你们还好吗？行吗？好的。

  - [18:21 - 18:34] **Class Challenge: Starting Technology Intelligence from Scratch**

    Now coming back to the lecture, any questions so far? And whatever we covered, can we move on to today's topic? Okay, here's the challenge. And whoever can answer this need not write the logbook for the day. And whoever is able to answer this at the end of the class can get full marks for the discussion today—not the logbook waiver, but only because if you don't know, at least you listen and then try to answer at the end of the class.

    Now what is the challenge? You know what is foresighting. You know it is valuable, but you know it is challenging as well to do. But if you were to start intelligence and foresighting activities in your organization from scratch—imagine you are in a company wherein there's nothing being done and you want to start this intelligence and foresighting from scratch—how would you go about doing it? What steps would you take? Don't rush and take some time. For those who have seen through the lecture notes or read some articles, you might be able to answer somewhat. But for those who haven't, it's still okay, you can give it a shot.

    *Student:* Yeah actually I think personally for me I think incentives and benefits are one of the key important things when it comes to...

    *Professor:* Okay, incentives and approval from laws and buy-in from boss, okay, right. These are two things, okay?

    *Student:* I would actually set up a scout team, so...

    *Professor:* Okay, have some scouts and have under this... Okay. Think holistically. Okay. Yes.

    So I'm not giving it to you. I'll give it to this time, okay? So Channel not for Meredith, okay? The important thing is... many of you talked about what is going on here.     It's like, you know, the excellent example given is: all you said is spot on correct. But they are like... if you take... if there is something you're driving, if there is something wrong in a car, right, you would go to a mechanic shop, a technician.     So the way it works is... that's where the theory comes in. You have the systemic and systematic way to understand TI system which comprises of... This is the actual process: formulation of what we want. You understand your power zones, you look at outside industry where technology is going, you collect information, analyze it, and then finally you make decisions based on it. This is what is the important step I don't... but for it to function well, you need something above it, which is **TI Management**. What's the mission and goal? How do you structure the whole activity and what are the tools? Some of you said, right? "Oh my God, what methodology might work?" All these things need to be known. So like what Sohail said, right, first comes the **TI Management**, which is all about **Designing, Directing, and Developing**.

    What's in there? So if I'm working for a company, first I need to... what is **Designing**? Creating a theoretical model which reflects a "yet to be constructed reality." Which means if I were to start foresighting or intelligence activity for my company, what form should it take? For that, you need to understand what industry you're operating in. What is the culture of the company? Is it top-down driven or bottom-up driven? Are they in the forefront of innovation or not? Are they followers? But you move down... you had the bigger picture, but you move down immediately. It's very similar to top-down vision. Once you have that, now designing done. Okay. For my organization, given its hierarchical nature, probably a TI and you know, an **Intelligence of the Organization** is better a top-down approach wherein the boss says, you know, gives that question, throws it in, the analyst just collects the data and gives it and decision making happens at the high level. Is that part... that is the designing part.

    Then comes **Directing** wherein you Orient your system so that it fulfills the mission. You understand what is the mission. You need to ensure that top gives the right question to the analyst. You hire analysts, you have proper people designated to it. Give them tools, give them resources. Convince your boss to allocate resources. So directing happens there.

    And one part which Meredith did not say is that probably it doesn't happen. It happens, but the government organizations don't do it. Higher education institutions... once you have conceptualized, once you have directed it, it will function. But it need not always function correctly. Something could go wrong and it requires improvement. So as a person initiating the Intelligence or Foresighting, you need to go and continuously improve. Right, someone said knowledge management. Yes, it is important. Who said knowledge management? That's it, Knowledge sharing, someone here, right? Yeah. So knowledge management is important, but even when knowledge management is implemented, there is a person constantly studying the knowledge management systems to see if it's working well or not. I'll cover in the Knowledge Management class—if you're not constantly developing it, it will fail big time. So there is a need for the person who's orchestrating the whole thing to Design, Direct, and not only that, constantly monitor to upgrade it. That is **Developing**.

    Okay, and what is Design, Directing, and Developing is all about understanding your industry, organization, what culture works. Am I going to organize? What structure is it going to take? Is it going to be bottom-up or top-down? Do we have the necessary culture like that? If not, put that culture in place. Everything has to be done by this person. Now, this happens in again, you know, organizations which take foresight and intelligence very seriously. It's not shown to outsiders. Someone said—Karthik mentioned about "black box." Yes, it's kept as a black box because they don't want all the information to be available to every employee because they can easily replicate in our organization. That's why it's kept as a black box. Now for this you need somebody who is capable of doing. Is that clear?

    所以我没有给你们答案。我这次给，好吗？所以 Meredith 没拿到分，好吗？重要的事情是……你们很多人谈到了这里发生了什么。“情报”意味着像 Karishma 说的，对吧？你去搜索，你过滤，你分析，你们有些人说，“噢不，应该与自上而下的需求保持一致，”等等等等。而且“否则，就没有整合。它行不通。”所有这些都很重要，但这是实际发生的活动。关键温度。

    但是如果你要开始，这非常类似于……我喜欢一位教授的这个例子，他过去常说你为什么需要……为什么你应该知道理论？很多学生讨厌理论，对吧？他们想要非常有经验的技能型知识。我们为什么要学理论？为什么要尝试？所有的学者都努力看案例，并试图从中提炼理论。为了什么目的？他们没有更好的工作吗？理论的目的是什么？

    这就像，你知道，给出的那个绝佳例子是：你们说的全都完全正确。但它们就像……如果你拿……如果你在开车，如果车子出了毛病，对吧，你会去修车店，找技师。他知道哪里出了问题，他会修好车，然后你就可以开了，对吧。但是如果你让技师从头设计一辆车，他能做到吗？不是所有人都能做到。好的，所以为此需要适当的基础学位建设。这就是为什么理论非常重要。所以你们所有的答案都是正确的。但是如果你要在组织中开始情报和预见活动，有一种方法可以做到，这是相似的，因为她来自公共部门，她有更广阔的视野，我们应该这样做。

    所以它的工作方式是……这就是理论发挥作用的地方。你有系统性和体系化的方式来理解 TI 系统，它包括……这是实际的过程：制定我们想要什么。你了解你的权力区域（Power Zones），你观察外部行业技术的发展方向，你收集信息，分析它，最后基于此做出决策。这是重要的一步，我不……但为了让它运作良好，你需要在这个之上的东西，那就是 **TI 管理（TI Management）**。使命和目标是什么？你如何构建整个活动，工具有哪些？你们有些人说了，对吧？“天哪，什么方法论可能有效？”所有这些事情都需要知道。所以像 Sohail 说的，对吧，首先是 **TI 管理**，这全是关于 **设计（Designing）、指导（Directing）和发展（Developing）**。

    里面有什么？如果我在为一家公司工作，首先我需要……什么是 **设计**？创建一个反映“尚未构建的现实”的理论模型。这意味着如果我要为我的公司开始预见或情报活动，它应该采取什么形式？为此，你需要了解你在什么行业运营。公司的文化是什么？是自上而下的还是自下而上的？他们是否处于创新的前沿？他们是追随者吗？但你往下移……你有了大局，但你立刻往下移。这非常类似于自上而下的愿景。一旦你有了那个，好了，设计完成。好的。对于我的组织，鉴于其等级性质，可能 TI 和你知道的，**组织的智能（Intelligence of the Organization）** 最好是自上而下的方法，老板说，你知道，给出那个问题，抛进去，分析师只是收集数据并给出结果，决策发生在高层。那是那部分……那就是设计部分。

    然后是 **指导**，你需要以此为导向调整你的系统，以便它履行使命。你理解使命是什么。你需要确保高层给分析师正确的问题。你雇佣分析师，你有适当的人员指定去做。给他们工具，给他们资源。说服你的老板分配资源。所以指导发生在那里。

    Meredith 没有提到的一部分是，可能它不会发生。它发生了，但政府组织不这样做。高等教育机构……一旦你概念化了，一旦你指导了它，它就会运作。但它不一定总是正确运作。可能会出问题，需要改进。所以作为发起情报或预见的人，你需要去不断改进。对，有人说知识管理。是的，这很重要。谁说的知识管理？就是那个，知识共享，这里有人说的，对吧？是的。所以知识管理很重要，但即使实施了知识管理，也需要有人不断研究知识管理系统，看看它是否运行良好。我会在知识管理课上讲——如果你不不断发展它，它将会惨败。所以需要有人来协调整个事情，去设计、指导，不仅如此，还要不断监控以升级它。这就是 **发展**。

    好的，设计、指导和发展全是关于了解你的行业、组织、什么文化有效。我要组织吗？它将采取什么结构？是自下而上还是自上而下？我们要有那样必要的文化吗？如果没有，就把那种文化建立起来。一切都必须由这个人来做。现在，这再次发生在，你知道，那些非常认真对待预见和情报的组织里。它不向外人展示。有人说——Karthik 提到了“黑匣子”。是的，它作为一个黑匣子被保留，因为他们不想让所有的信息对每个员工都可用，因为他们可以在我们的组织中轻易复制。这就是为什么它被保留为黑匣子。现在为此你需要有能力做这件事的人。清楚了吗？

  - [18:41 - 19:02] **Debate: Intelligence of the Organization vs. Organizational Intelligence**

    This is the first step. And it requires the next step, that is TI management happening. The person who takes charge, he needs to understand, and everyone needs to understand the mission and goals of TI. And what are they? As I mentioned earlier. Many of you mentioned this, right? Yeah, Karishma mentioned this analyst was working on something. It wasn't integrated with the decision maker. There was a big gap. Quite a few mentioned that.

    So what happens is whether it's top-down or bottom-up, it doesn't matter. You need to understand TI as an activity conducted by a set of agents or can be viewed as a product with customers. Who are the customers in for TI? Decision makers are the customers. And who is the person making the product here? Analysts, okay. So it's something like there are two customers, two people here, two stakeholders here in TI: one is the **Intelligence Consumer** who are decision makers, other one is **Intelligence Broker**, who are typically the analysts.

    And intelligence can happen both ways in a company. Either it's top-down or bottom-up. What does top-down mean? The decision makers will tell you: "You know what I want to know about answers to these?" They will formulate the question and throw to you. "What is the next technology that I need to implement in NUS?" **Karthik** is asking those questions because he's directing a data... I want to... But what about this? You and I do the job and we tell the Proctor, "Maybe you want to know this because here is AI coming and we use the other organization like this. Maybe do this," okay, and he will decide ultimately. But it's your job to push it up.

    But culture is very important and you also pointed out because why this happens and it doesn't get pushed up. It gets lost especially by middle managers. You will read an article where it shows middle managers are the culprit many a times with the information not going up. For that to function well, you need to have certain culture within the organization, which we'll see later. Okay, so it can be either bottom-up or top-down. Bottom-up meaning: "What do we need to know? Why do we need to know? Decision is to be made once we know it," so you're pushing that information back to the decision makers.

    Either way it's bottom-up or top-down. You need to understand okay this is where I want to deliberate on if it is top-down driven.     这是第一步。并且它需要下一步，那就是 TI 管理的发生。负责的人，他需要理解，每个人都需要理解 TI 的使命和目标。它们是什么？正如我之前提到的。你们很多人提到了这个，对吧？是的，Karishma 提到这个分析师在做某事。它没有与决策者整合。有一个很大的差距。相当多的人提到了这一点。

    所以发生的情况是，不管是自上而下还是自下而上，这都不重要。你需要将 TI 理解为由一组代理人进行的活动，或者可以看作是带有客户的产品。TI 的客户是谁？决策者是客户。谁是这里制造产品的人？分析师，好的。所以这就像在 TI 中有两个客户，两个人，两个利益相关者：一个是 **情报消费者（Intelligence Consumer）**，即决策者；另一个是 **情报经纪人（Intelligence Broker）**，通常是分析师。

    情报在公司里可以通过两种方式发生。要么是自上而下，要么是自下而上。自上而下是什么意思？决策者会告诉你：“你知道我想知道这些问题的答案吗？”他们会制定问题并抛给你。“我需要在新加坡国立大学（NUS）实施的下一项技术是什么？” **Karthik** 问这些问题是因为他在指导一个数据……我想……但这又怎么样呢？你和我做这份工作，我们告诉考官（Proctor），“也许你想知道这个，因为 AI 来了，我们像这样使用其他组织。也许这样做，”好的，他最终会决定。但你的工作是把它推上去。

    但文化非常重要，你也指出了为什么这会发生以及为什么它没有被推上去。它经常在中层管理者那里丢失。你会读到一篇文章，其中显示中层管理者很多时候是信息不上达的罪魁祸首。为了让它运作良好，你需要在组织内有特定的文化，我们稍后会看到。好的，所以它可以是自下而上或自上而下。自下而上的意思是：“我们需要知道什么？为什么我们需要知道？一旦我们知道就要做决定，”所以你把那个信息推回给决策者。

    无论哪种方式，自下而上或自上而下。你需要理解，好的，这就是我要斟酌的地方，如果是自上而下驱动的。这意味着决策者说“这是一个问题”，他把它给 Kai 说“去分析并给我数据，向我展示发现，因为我需要做决定。”那么他就成了 **组织的智能（Intelligence of the Organization）**。对。这就是为什么它被称为组织的智能。Karthik 现在是组织的智能，因为管理层明确制定了他们需要采取行动的情报需求。他们推动它，他们收集数据，分析，然后把发现给老板。

    但另一方面，像你们有些人说的，如果是自下而上的，这意味着整个组织表现得像 **组织性智能（Organizational Intelligence）**，这意味着什么？当我做我的工作时，我偶然发现某些事情，我像他说的那样拿起范围。我是侦察员。我收集信息，然后如果我看到它很紧迫，我就把它传递下去。所以每个人，整个组织，都变成了情报分析师。

    现在哪种方法好？你说了什么吗？好答案。让我们稍微斟酌一下，好吗？与其一对一，不如我们在班上辩论一下，好吗。这个班的一半会说……我们正好分在这里。你的名字是……数10个，然后这后面的是半个班，好的。这是另一半班。所以这个班要讲为什么 **组织的智能（自上而下）** 是好的，这个班有两个不同的……为什么整个组织变得智能（**组织性智能**）更好？好的。不要重复观点。因为你们的课堂参与可能会变，已经告诉过你们这取决于发言次数。对我来说，这不重要，好吗？所以要增加价值。有人说了一个观点，你否定它并用你的论点证实。好的，所以现在开始。让我们从这里开始。为什么你认为组织的智能是好的？

    *学生：* 是的。我认为阅读材料也提到，有时底层人员只是……信息量巨大，他们不知道什么是重要的。通过自上而下，你给那些人一个指令，比如……你给他们寻找的范围。也许你资源有限，人员有限，所以你可以缩小范围，针对对你的战略、你的公司更重要的事情。是的，因为外面的信息是巨大的。

    *学生（Karishma）：* 是的，有可能混乱，但我认为仅仅因为有很多信息，并不一定总是导致所有信息只是坐在那里成为很多噪音。我认为随着它得到更多的探索——因为当你有自上而下时，它可能会回到之前的确认偏见点，因为他们基于他们所知道的和现有的商业模式行动——但因为有更多的信息让他们在之后的理解和战略上行动，这将引起更多的基于场景的思考或多重解释。所以我不认为它只会……会有混乱，因为有很多信息。因为有很多信息，每个人都贡献更多，可能会出现重叠，当这种重叠出现时，他们可以意识到因为所有这些信息，什么是更紧迫的问题。它可以被更好地重新构建。

    *教授：* 再一次，你的观点没有被完全接受。所以你应该说：“是的，这会是混乱的，但如果你有适当的结构来剔除多余的信息，剔除噪音但取出有价值的输入。”你如何做到这一点？如果噪音太大，这是一个非常有效的观点。但如果你聪明的话，你仍然可以从自下而上的方法中提取有价值的信息？两件事。你怎么感觉？那是个问题。你有什么过程来过滤知识？

    *学生：* 设定目标。

    *教授：* 那是其中之一。这被称为结果导向的方法，确认。那就是知识管理发挥作用的地方。为了去除过滤信息，特别是，你知道，将有价值的东西与噪音分开，你需要有适当的知识管理实践。其中一种方法被称为结果导向的方法，我会讲到。

    *学生：* 有一件事我们应该放在第一位，就是如果我们建立某个东西，每个人都走同样的路，那是同一个方向。如果每个人都走不同的方向，那将是一团糟。就像我们在建一座教堂。自下而上，每个人的目标是我们正在建教堂，而不是修路。我们在种树或我们在盖楼。不，我们在建教堂。每个人都有相同的目标，所以哪一个是优先事项，哪一个是实践，因为有太多的方向被谈论……无论是长期还是短期。它应该被讨论。我们可以为每个人的精力做什么，我们可以贡献更多，我们有新想法。这也是自上而下的做法，即新产品、孵化，也许很多人，他们有新想法，他们有很多好想法。公司组织应该做的是他们……活动去喜欢个人，初级人员分享他们的想法如何让这些事情变得更好，然后决定也是自上而下的。他们将决定哪一个将是更好的方式，最好的一个。他们会选择最好的一个来点亮它，我们意识到这样就不会是一团糟。所以完全不管是长期还是短期，个人贡献还是组织贡献，都是自上而下的。决定全是自上而下的。

    *教授：* 你知道有一个很好的，但也有一个更好的攻击方法。她的观点是……

    *教授：* 那么你必须说的是，是的，所有的结构……然后你的观点可以是：“是的，你有这些多样化的人去侦察并获取信息，有结构，一切都有，但当信息被喂给老板时，他是如此糟糕的一个老板，他随意行动。他无视一切。”那又怎样？一切都浪费了。你在说的是，无论如何，这是自上而下驱动的，对吧？他做决定，这是你在说的。

    但是有一件事你知道，有一件事你没有提到关于另一件事的缺点是，如果他们要成为情报人员，对吧？他们真的能那样工作吗？这会发生吗？为什么他用那个作为反击？是的，这很好。但他们如何管理他们的日常工作并成为情报分析师，对吧？他们如何兼顾？很好。你们说的都很好，但他们能做到吗？你们中有多少人真正做着照顾日常琐事的工作，同时又是情报官？这不容易管理，这就是为什么你需要有什么？

    所以这场辩论的总结是什么？所以他们只是做情报，而不做工作。资源——一个是情报，对吧？激励，对吧，即使是自下而上的，你确保这就是为什么你知道在研发密集型行业，他们给一天假来做宠物项目。对。他们给 20% 的时间。他们为什么那样做？你的工作是像个人教授一样工作，在一些公司，他们送你去参加会议、贸易展。他们为什么那样做？为了让你去享受，你知道，维也纳，然后回来？这是其中的一部分。但你去参加，把信息带回组织，对吧？所以它是行得通的，但需要有适当的文化和单一结构才能使其运作。如果它在适当的结构下运作良好，那是最好的部分。对，但这说起来容易做起来难。这就是为什么任何一种方法都可能有效，但这取决于你在看的组织。

    这就是为什么当我说 **设计** 一个 TI 系统时，当然，首先要了解你的公司。你是什么？如果你像哈雷戴维森，对吧，当然他们有最酷的摩托车。但你知道像……我说是你做的那些组织有多僵化。那是他们在那里拥有的一种结构。自下而上的方法行不通。它……它适用于更具创业精神、对新想法持开放态度的公司。好的，所以首先你需要了解我有什么样的公司心态，我的老板是谁？我是否有一种文化，如果我给员工自由时间，他们会做工作[而不是]搭便车，对吧？我的员工是否有很高的积极性？我的激励结构是否照顾到了这一点？很多事情需要评估。这就是为什么管理整件事的人需要了解理论框架中的一切，以便设计出适当的系统。而对于自下而上要起作用，最重要的事情之一，我想在下一张幻灯片里也有。我会给你们看，好的。

    现在问题来了……不管你，你的公司是采用自上而下的方法还是自下而上的方法——自下而上的方法也是一样——利益相关者双方都应该要么提出一个问题，要么试图提出一些数据、分析和发现来回答一个问题，对吧？因为最终每个人都要回答一个问题。这样才能做出决策。

    现在，如果是自上而下……如果是自上而下，更容易。因为老板知道公司要去哪里，也许是对的，或者他可能是错的，但如果他说，“嘿，去告诉我这项技术什么时候会进入市场。它成熟需要多长时间？”这就是一个明确的问题。那么对于美国的分析师来说，这更容易开展工作；你会开始获取关于谁在研究这项技术、专利是什么样的、进展如何、TRL（技术就绪指数）是什么样的数据，等等。你会着手去做。对。如果你是自下而上的，我的信息也……你会寻找可能回答某些问题的信息，即：“与这项技术相比，这项技术的表现是否更优越？”当然，然后你比较性能，然后推送它。你是老板，对吧？

    无论哪种方式，问题是两种方法产生巨大差异的原因。这就是为什么当你看这个过程时……虽然过程是一样的，但差距是什么？需求是什么？情报混合回答的问题是什么？这对你的预见是否对你有利有着天壤之别。所以归根结底，你会提出什么样的问题……如果你是利益相关者之一，无论是老板还是分析师……我已经告诉过你：“这项技术什么时候成熟？”这是一个。记得 **谁、什么、何时、为什么、如何** 吗？它们在定义事物吗？你能提出哪些其他的面向未来的问题？是的。

    *学生：* 就受监管的行业而言，例如银行业，是的，因为他们不能总是直接跳上新型技术，但我猜他们的 TI 版本会是：“这种类型是不可避免的吗？就像我们必须非常顺应这个吗？你认为政策会允许我采用这项技术并为了我的战略优势实施它吗？”

    *教授：* 这是一个非常重要的问题。政策没有跟上技术的步伐……你去投资它是没用的，对吧？这很多时候都在发生。知道为什么区块链和所有东西……周围有那么多不确定性，因为……不，即使，你知道，自动驾驶汽车，有那么多不确定性，因为政策还没有到位。保险政策和一切。它还没有生效。是的。还有什么？

    *学生：* 还有谁这件事做得很好？还有谁在押注这项技术？或者甚至是谁在开发这项技术？

    *教授：* 我对那家公司有信心吗？如果是家初创公司，它倒闭了，那我该怎么办？还有什么？

    *学生：* 一旦……会有什么可观察到的增量（Delta）……？在节省方面？

    *教授：* 很好。好处会是什么，对吧？你能量化它吗？采用这项技术？是的。

    *学生：* 实施需要多长时间？

    *教授：* 很好。这是一个非常实际的挑战。需要多长时间？我能一直从口袋里亏钱直到那时候吗？直到所有的好处出现？直到我熬过去？是的。

    *学生：* 它有风险吗？

    *教授：* 风险评估，好的。

    *学生：* 我们对这个新测试了解多少，我们对它了解多少，它将花费多少，这些也是重要的方面。事实上，如果是 IT 公司，总部的利益相关者会被问到比如未来五年什么技术会流行，谁会是这项技术的赢家，我们在哪里，我们的位置，我们目前站在哪里，我们如何能升至顶端，也许前三或第一，我们应该何时采取某些特定活动以确保我们上升？这将是我们公司的综合问题集。

    *教授：* 知道他们知道什么？那是二乘二图表之一。那就是知识管理发挥作用的地方。是的，你知道，你们都说对了关于 **Praveen Linga** 和天然气水合物等等。在某一点上，当然我们有良好的知识管理系统。在 NUS 的某一点上，如果外部利益相关者来问“嘿，我想解决这个问题，”可能是能源部门什么的，我们会争先恐后地找谁是这里的专家，对吧，直到我们有了适当的知识管理系统。好的，你一说这个，我就知道 **Praveen Linga** 能做，对吧？所以那全是关于知识管理，是的。

    *学生：* 它如何适应我们要公司？然后是否有任何额外的事情我们需要做来让公司为此做好准备？

    *教授：* 是的，我们如何适应？怎么没人谈论政府和资金，尽管有一个来自安全部门，一个来自政府部门？是的，是的。

    *学生：* 更像是，现在采用这个的时机对吗？呃，对我个人而言……

    *教授：* 有时，时间是杀手。这是采用它的正确时机吗？我什么时候需要跳进去？这总是一个非常棘手的挑战。是的。

    *学生：* 有没有其他公司你想收购，或者你想等待，就像你说的，等到技术出现，还是你想现在就开始投资？你想做什么样的决定？

    *教授：* 很好。你知道，所有这些都是至关重要的决定。噢不，你错过了大部分重要的东西。所以回到你，先生们，对吧？

    *学生：* 政府是否提供补贴来支持我们做这个？

    *教授：* 谁在给我补贴？有政府拨款吗？那是我们做预见时应该总是留意的第一件事，因为你在押注未来，这是非常不确定的，你应该利用什么是……我如何减轻我的……浪费资源的浪费。我能拉谁入伙？谁会资助？这在很多行业都在大规模发生，尤其是当我们提出……知道五年计划时，所有的公司都在看……对。什么是……现在的钱路在哪里，对吧？所有的行业，然后公司开始运作。我提出我们应该做什么的建议。

    是的。所以为了节省时间，我们继续。这些是……要么是最高管理层，要么当你作为分析师时可以问的一组问题。你们错过的一些事情也与 **知识产权（IP）** 保护有关。应该追求什么形式的与该技术相关的知识产权保护？这也取决于你的行业，如果有……IT 保护有时不起作用，好的。市场你可以考虑，你知道这就是为什么如果你看这个图表……无论是自上而下还是自下而上，你基本上都在问这个问题：谁、什么、然后、然后为什么？你应该谈论 **政治、经济、社会、技术、法律和环境（PESTLE）** 的所有方面。虽然他们称活动为市场、竞争和技术，但它不仅限于此。它涉及更广泛的社会经济、环境——对于能源行业的人来说更是如此。好的，那些来自能源系统的人，更广泛的环境构成了一幅图景。

So they think having the data centers there will give them the... they can kind of protect the data is what they think, and they have massively... Data is going to be the way the world goes. They didn't want to take it out as well, they say. But is it worth the investment? It's a... it's a foresight product on its own. A country betting on such a big initiative when there is power cut, when there is water shortage, when there is so much of environmental concerns.     But these are things which you need to watch out for, right?
